2:28:1707:0:14:72:506:700:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:?:Controlling for the Compromise Effect Debiases Estimates of Risk Preference Parameters 
2:29:1793:14:14:72:511:686:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:?:Jonathan P. Beauchamp, Daniel J. Benjamin, Christopher F. Chabris, and David I. Laibson 
2:30:1880:14:14:72:234:672:12:Times:12:Times:?:NBER Working Paper No. 21792
2:31:1908:14:14:72:273:658:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:?:December 2015, Revised November 2018
2:32:1944:14:28:71:229:644:12:Times:12:Times:?:JEL No. B49,D03,D14,D83,G11
2:33:1971:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:?:ABSTRACT
2:34:1979:28:14:71:539:588:12:Times:12:Times:?:The compromise effect arises when options near the "middle" of a choice set are more appealing. The
2:35:2078:14:14:71:539:574:12:Times:12:Times:?:compromise effect poses conceptual and practical problems for economic research: by influencing
2:36:2173:14:14:71:539:560:12:Times:12:Times:?:choices, it distorts revealed preferences, biasing researchers' inferences about deep (i.e., domain general)
2:37:2281:14:14:71:539:546:12:Times:12:Times:?:preferences. We propose and estimate an econometric model that disentangles and identifies both deep
2:38:2381:14:14:71:539:532:12:Times:12:Times:?:preferences and the context-dependent compromise effect. We demonstrate our method using data
2:39:2474:14:14:71:539:518:12:Times:12:Times:?:from an experiment with 550 participants who made choices over lotteries from multiple price lists.
2:40:2573:14:14:71:539:504:12:Times:12:Times:?:Following prior work, we manipulate the compromise effect by varying the middle options of each
2:41:2668:14:14:71:539:490:12:Times:12:Times:?:multiple price list and then estimate risk preferences without modelling the compromise effect. These
2:42:2769:14:14:71:539:476:12:Times:12:Times:?:naïve parameter estimates are not robust: they change as the compromise effect is manipulated. To
2:43:2866:14:14:71:539:462:12:Times:12:Times:?:eliminate this bias, we incorporate the compromise effect directly into our econometric model. We
2:44:2963:14:14:71:539:448:12:Times:12:Times:?:show that this method generates robust estimates of risk preference parameters that are no longer sensitive
2:45:3070:14:14:71:539:434:12:Times:12:Times:?:to compromise-effect manipulations. This method can be applied to other settings that exhibit the compromise
2:46:3178:14:42:71:101:420:12:Times:12:Times:?:effect.
2:47:3185:42:14:71:186:378:12:Times:12:Times:?:Jonathan P. Beauchamp
2:48:3206:14:14:71:197:364:12:Times:12:Times:?:Department of Economics
2:49:3229:14:14:71:164:350:12:Times:12:Times:?:Harvard University
2:50:3247:14:14:71:183:336:12:Times:12:Times:?:Cambridge, MA 02138
2:51:3266:14:28:71:232:322:12:Times:12:Times:?:beauchamp.jonathan@gmail.com
2:52:3294:28:14:71:163:294:12:Times:12:Times:?:Daniel J. Benjamin
2:53:3312:14:14:71:277:280:12:Times:12:Times:?:Center for Economics and Social Research
2:54:3352:14:14:71:233:266:12:Times:12:Times:?:University of Southern California

3:71:3685:0:33:72:201:709:206:g_d0_f2:206:g_d0_f2:?:1Introduction
3:72:3698:33:22:88:540:676:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Thecompromise e§ectarises when options in a choice set can be ordered on common dimensions
3:73:3788:22:22:72:540:654:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:or attributes (such as price, quantity, size, or intensity), and decision makers tend to select the
3:74:3887:22:22:72:540:632:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:options in the ìmiddleî of the choice set.  For example, suppose a group of respondents were
3:75:3979:22:22:72:540:610:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:asked whether they wanted a free nature hike of either 1 mile or 4 miles.  Now suppose that a
3:76:4072:22:22:72:540:588:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:di§erent, otherwise identical group were asked whether they preferred a free nature hike of 1, 4, or
3:77:4172:22:22:72:540:566:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:7 miles. A compromise e§ect could lead to agreaterfraction of respondents choosing 4 miles in
3:78:4265:22:22:72:540:544:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the second choice set (see Simonson 1989 for a closely related empirical result and Kamenica 2008
3:79:4362:22:22:72:251:522:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:for a discussion of microfoundations).
3:80:4400:22:21:88:539:500:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The compromise e§ect poses conceptual and practical problems for economic research. By ináu-
3:81:4492:21:22:72:540:479:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:encing choices, the compromise e§ect distorts revealed preferences, biasing researchersí inferences
3:82:4591:22:22:72:290:457:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:about deep (i.e., domain general) preferences.
3:83:4637:22:22:88:539:435:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In this paper, we propose and estimate an econometric model that disentangles and separately
3:84:4729:22:22:72:540:413:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:identiÖesboththe deep preferences and the (situational) compromise e§ect that is ináuencing
3:85:4820:22:22:72:540:391:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the expression of those deep preferences. To demonstrate our approach, we conduct a laboratory
3:86:4914:22:22:72:540:369:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:experiment with 550 participants in which we elicit risk preferences using a multiple price list
3:87:5010:22:22:72:540:347:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(MPL). We study this context because, despite the limitations of the MPL procedure, it is among
3:88:5105:22:22:72:540:325:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the most commonly used methods to elicit preferences in the economics literature (e.g., Tversky and
3:89:5204:22:22:72:540:303:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Kahneman 1992, Holt and Laury 2002, Harrison, List, and Towe 2007, Andersen, Harrison, Lau,
3:90:5295:22:22:72:540:281:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:and Rutstrˆm 2008) and because the compromise e§ect has been carefully and robustly documented
3:91:5389:22:22:72:540:259:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:already in the context of inferring risk preferences using an MPL (Birnbaum 1992, Harrison, Lau,
3:92:5485:22:22:72:540:237:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Rutstrˆm, and Sullivan 2005, Andersen, Harrison, Lau, and Rutstrˆm 2006, Harrison, Lau, and
3:93:5576:22:21:72:540:215:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Rutstrˆm 2007). We use the term compromise e§ect as short-hand for a bias toward the middle
3:94:5667:21:22:72:291:194:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:option, which is what these papers document.
3:95:5711:22:22:88:539:172:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The screenshot below is drawn from our own experiment and is typical of MPL experiments.
3:96:5799:22:22:72:540:150:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In this example, a participant is asked to make seven binary choices. Each of the seven choices is
3:97:5897:22:22:72:540:128:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:between a gamble and a sure-thing alternative. The gamble doesnít change across the seven rows,
3:98:5992:22:64:72:339:106:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:while the sure-thing alternative varies from high to low.
3:99:6049:64:0:303:308:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:3
4:100:6050:0:22:88:539:556:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:A subject who displayed a strong compromise e§ect would act as if she were indi§erent between
4:101:6143:22:22:72:540:534:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the gamble and the sure-thing in the middle row, which is row (d). Such indi§erence would imply
4:102:6238:22:22:72:540:512:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:that she is risk seeking because the gamble has a lower expected value than the sure thing in row
4:103:6335:22:22:72:540:490:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(d). In this example, a strong compromise e§ect would lead a participant who may otherwise be
4:104:6428:22:22:72:262:468:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:risk-averse to make risk-seeking choices.
4:105:6469:22:22:88:539:446:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Following prior work (Birnbaum 1992, Harrison, Lau, Rutstrˆm, and Sullivan 2005, Andersen,
4:106:6559:22:22:72:540:424:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Harrison, Lau, and Rutstrˆm 2006, Harrison, Lau, and Rutstrˆm 2007, and Harrison, List, and
4:107:6650:22:22:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Towe 2007), we experimentally vary the middle option using scale manipulations. SpeciÖcally, we
4:108:6745:22:22:72:540:380:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:hold the lowest and highest alternatives of the MPL Öxed and manipulate the locations of the
4:109:6837:22:21:72:540:358:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Öve intermediate outcomes within the scale.  For example, compare the screenshot above to the
4:110:6930:21:22:72:540:337:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:screenshot that follows, which has new alternatives in rows (b) through (f), although rows (a) and
4:111:7028:22:22:72:540:315:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(g) are the same. With respect to this second MPL, an agent who acts as if the middle option, row
4:112:7125:22:178:72:373:293:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(d), is her indi§erence point would be judged to be risk averse.
4:113:7189:178:22:72:535:115:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In our experiment, each participant is exposed to one of Öve di§erent scale treatment conditions.
4:114:7286:22:51:88:539:93:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:To econometrically disentangle risk preferences from the compromise e§ect, we augment a
4:115:7373:51:0:303:308:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:4
5:116:7374:0:22:72:540:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:discrete-choice model with additional parameters that represent a penalty for choosing a switch
5:117:7469:22:22:72:540:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:point further from the middle. Note that our approach of incorporating the compromise e§ect into
5:118:7565:22:22:72:540:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the econometric model is di§erent from including treatment-condition indicators as controls. Sim-
5:119:7662:22:22:72:540:643:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:ply controlling for treatment condition would not identify domain-general preferences because the
5:120:7759:22:22:72:540:621:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:compromise e§ect can ináuence choices ineverytreatment condition (i.e., there is no benchmark,
5:121:7853:22:22:72:284:599:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:compromise-e§ect-free treatment condition).
5:122:7896:22:22:88:539:577:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The deep preferences we study in the current paper are prospect-theoretic preferences over risky
5:123:7992:22:22:72:540:555:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:lotteries (e.g., Tversky and Kahneman 1992, Wakker 2010, Bruhin, Fehr-Duda, and Epper 2010).
5:124:8084:22:22:72:540:533:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Our ex-ante hypotheses focus on two parameters: curvature!(which captures risk aversion over
5:125:8176:22:22:72:540:511:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:gains and risk seeking over losses) and loss aversion"(which captures the degree to which people
5:126:8272:22:22:72:462:489:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:dislike losses more than they like gains).1Our analysis yields three main Öndings.
5:127:8354:22:22:88:539:467:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:First, our estimates of the compromise-e§ect parameters replicate the Öndings from earlier
5:128:8444:22:21:72:540:445:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:work that participants have a bias toward choosing a switch point in the middle rows of the MPL
5:129:8539:21:22:72:540:424:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(e.g., Harrison, Lau, Rutstrˆm, and Sullivan 2005; see other references above).  Moreover, our
5:130:8633:22:22:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:quantitative estimates indicate that the bias is sizeable; we estimate that the attractiveness of the
5:131:8734:22:22:72:528:380:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:middle rows relative to the extreme rows represents 17%-23% of the prospectsí monetary value.
5:132:8827:22:22:88:539:358:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Second, when we estimate the prospect-theory model without controls for the compromise e§ect,
5:133:8920:22:22:72:540:336:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the scale manipulations have a very powerful e§ect on the (mis-) estimated preference parameters.
5:134:9017:22:22:72:540:314:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In particular, the compromise e§ect is strong enough to cause us to estimate either risk seeking
5:135:9113:22:22:72:540:292:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(as predicted by prospect theory) or risk aversion (the opposite of what is predicted by prospect
5:136:9210:22:22:72:540:270:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:theory) in the loss domain, depending on the scale manipulations. The compromise e§ect is also
5:137:9304:22:22:72:540:248:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:strong enough that, when manipulated, it can make behavior look as if there is essentially no loss
5:138:9402:22:22:72:342:226:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:aversion (see the results for the Pull 2 treatment below).
5:139:9460:22:22:88:539:204:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Third, when we estimate the prospect-theory parameters while including additional parameters
5:140:9552:22:22:72:540:182:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to capture the compromise e§ect, our estimates of!and"are robust across the Öve scale treatment
5:141:9647:22:15:72:540:160:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:conditions. The robustness of these preference-parameter estimates implies that they are not biased
5:142:9746:15:15:84:539:145:36:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:?:1We predicted that our scaling manipulations would not substantially change the estimated parameters of the
5:143:9853:15:11:72:540:130:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:probability weighting function, because the prospects all have ìprobability-áippedî variants:  i.e., for each MPL
5:144:9966:11:11:72:542:119:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:featuring a prospect with probabilitypof monetary outcomexHand probability1!pof monetary outcomexL,the
5:145:10068:11:11:72:540:108:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:experiment includes another MPL featuring a probability-áipped prospect with probability1!pof outcomexHand
5:146:10174:11:11:72:545:97:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:probabilitypof outcomexL.Scalingmanipulationswillhave(approximately)o§settinge§ectswithrespecttothe
5:147:10273:11:44:72:370:86:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:probability weighting function for these two probability-áipped prospects.
5:148:10347:44:0:303:308:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:5
6:149:10348:0:22:72:540:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:by the compromise e§ect. (When estimating the model pooling all of our experimental data, our
6:150:10441:22:22:72:540:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:estimates are^!=0:24and^"=1:31, which fall within the range of estimates in the existing
6:151:10529:22:22:72:357:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:literature, albeit with^"toward the lower end of the range.)
6:152:10589:22:22:88:539:643:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In addition to the scale manipulations described above, we also study the e§ect of telling ex-
6:153:10683:22:22:72:540:621:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:perimental participants the expected value of the risky prospects. We hypothesized that this ma-
6:154:10779:22:22:72:540:599:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:nipulation would anchor the participants on the expected value, thereby nudging their preferences
6:155:10876:22:22:72:540:577:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:toward risk neutrality. However, we Önd that expected value information does not a§ect measured
6:156:10971:22:22:72:540:555:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:risk aversion nor measured loss aversion. Our null e§ect echoes the Öndings of Lichtenstein, Slovic,
6:157:11071:22:22:72:540:533:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:and Zink (1969) and Montgomery and Adelbratt (1982). However, Harrison and Rutstrˆm (2008)
6:158:11161:22:22:72:540:511:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:do Önd that providing expected value information signiÖcantly decreases risk aversion.  The dif-
6:159:11257:22:22:72:540:489:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:ference may arise because the prospects in their experiment are relatively complex, each involving
6:160:11355:22:22:72:367:467:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:four possible outcomes (versus one or two in our experiment).
6:161:11416:22:21:88:539:445:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:This paper contributes to the literature on the compromise e§ect by estimating a model that
6:162:11507:21:22:72:540:424:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:explicitly accounts for the compromise e§ect and enables us to separately estimate it from risk
6:163:11602:22:22:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:preferences.  Our sample is substantially larger than those used in earlier work, which allows us
6:164:11699:22:22:72:540:380:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to precisely estimate the e§ects of the scale manipulations. Moreover, because we pose gambles
6:165:11793:22:22:72:540:358:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:involving losses as well as gambles involving gains, we can study the e§ect of scale manipulations
6:166:11891:22:22:72:540:336:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:not only on risk aversion over gains, but also on risk aversion over losses and on loss aversion.
6:167:11988:22:22:72:540:314:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In addition, we provide estimates of the economic magnitude and importance of the compromise
6:168:12080:22:22:72:540:292:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:e§ect relative to the prospectsí monetary value, and we examine the demographic correlates of the
6:169:12177:22:22:72:540:270:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:parameters in our econometric model. A limitation of our experiment is that only one out of its
6:170:12272:22:22:72:540:248:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:four parts (which involves 28 of the 62 sets of choices we analyze) is incentivized. Reassuringly, all
6:171:12374:22:22:72:430:226:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:of our results still hold when we restrict attention to the incentivized data.
6:172:12452:22:22:88:539:204:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The rest of the paper is organized as follows. In Section 2, we discuss our experimental design.
6:173:12548:22:22:72:540:182:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In Section 3, we describe our econometric discrete-choice model, which incorporates the compromise
6:174:12646:22:21:72:540:160:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:e§ect. In Section 4, we list and discuss the Öve formal hypotheses that we test. In Section 5, we
6:175:12743:21:22:72:540:139:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:report the results of the estimation of our model, and we test the robustness of the estimates to the
6:176:12844:22:22:72:540:117:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:scale manipulations. Section 6 parallels Section 5 but examines the prospect-theory model without
6:177:12941:22:22:72:540:95:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:controls for the compromise e§ect. Section 7 estimates the economic magnitude and importance
6:178:13033:22:31:72:540:73:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:of the compromise e§ect in our data.  Sections 8 brieáy analyzes the demographic correlates of
6:179:13127:31:0:303:308:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:6
7:180:13128:0:22:72:539:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the main parameters of our econometric model (including!,", and parameters that capture the
7:181:13219:22:22:72:540:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:compromise e§ect).  Section 9 brieáy discusses the results of our expected value manipulation.
7:182:13313:22:46:72:173:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Section 10 concludes.
7:183:13334:46:33:72:195:619:206:g_d0_f2:206:g_d0_f2:?:2Experiment
7:184:13345:33:29:72:142:586:143:g_d0_f2:143:g_d0_f2:?:2.1  Design
7:185:13356:29:22:88:539:557:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Throughout the experiment, we employ the Multiple Price List (MPL) elicitation method (Tver-
7:186:13448:22:22:72:539:535:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f4:?:sky and Kahneman 1992, Holt and Laury 2002). At the top of each computer screen, aÖxed prospect
7:187:13543:22:22:72:540:513:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:is presented. The Öxed prospect is usually a non-degenerate lottery; it is ìÖxedî in the sense that
7:188:13642:22:22:72:539:491:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f4:?:it is an option in all of the binary choices on that screen.  (The Öxed prospect changesacross
7:189:13736:22:22:72:540:469:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:screens.) On each screen, seven binary choices are listed below the Öxed prospect. Each binary
7:190:13830:22:22:72:540:447:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:choice is made between the Öxed prospect (at the top of the screen) and what we refer to as an
7:191:13924:22:22:72:539:425:119:g_d0_f4:119:g_d0_f3:?:alternative(oralternative prospect). The alternatives vary within a screen, with one alternative
7:192:14020:22:21:72:540:403:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:for each of the seven binary choices. In some (but not all) cases, the alternatives are sure things.
7:193:14120:21:22:72:540:382:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Screenshots of the experiment are shown in the Introduction as well as in the Appendix, and the
7:194:14215:22:22:72:426:360:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:original instructions of the experiment are shown in the Online Appendix.
7:195:14288:22:22:88:539:338:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Our set-up for eliciting risk preferences is standard. Indeed, we designed many details of our
7:196:14382:22:22:72:540:316:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:experimentósuch as giving participants choices between a Öxed prospect and seven alternativesó
7:197:14476:22:22:72:540:294:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to closely follow Tversky and Kahnemanís (1992; henceforth T&K) experiment in their paper that
7:198:14570:22:22:72:540:272:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:introduced Cumulative Prospect Theory (CPT). Moreover, our set of Öxed prospects is identical
7:199:14663:22:22:72:540:250:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to the set used by T&K. Further mimicking T&Kís procedure, our computer program enforces
7:200:14751:22:22:72:540:228:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:consistency in the participantsí choices by requiring participants to respond monotonically to the
7:201:14849:22:22:72:540:206:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:seven choices on the screen.2Our algorithm for generating the seven alternatives is explained in
7:202:14945:22:22:72:540:184:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Section 2.2 and in the Online Appendix, where we also list the complete set of Öxed prospects and
7:203:15042:22:14:72:134:162:119:g_d0_f3:64:g_d0_f3:C:alternatives.3
7:204:15056:14:14:84:540:148:36:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:?:2More precisely, participants have to select only two circles: the one corresponding to the worst alternative outcome
7:205:15173:14:11:72:540:134:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:they prefer to the Öxed prospect and the one corresponding to the Öxed prospect in the following row. The other
7:206:15284:11:11:72:540:123:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:circles are auto-Ölled. This procedure is a version of the ìSwitching MPLî (or ìsMPLî, see Andersen et al. 2006).
7:207:15397:11:11:72:540:112:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:This procedure reduces participantsí fatigue and produces clean data, but it might have the unintended e§ect of
7:208:15508:11:7:72:465:101:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:biasing participants to select a row near the middle and thus exaggerating the compromise e§ect.
7:209:15604:7:15:84:540:94:36:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:?:3Our procedure di§ers from T&Kís in three ways. First, T&K do not report the actual values they used. Second,
7:210:15713:15:37:72:540:79:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:while their gambles were all hypothetical, our ìPart Aî gambles are incentivized.  Third, for each screen, T&K
7:211:15823:37:0:303:308:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:7
8:212:15824:0:22:88:539:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Each participant faces a total of 64 screens in the experiment, each of which contains seven
8:213:15916:22:22:72:540:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:choices between a Öxed prospect and alternatives. There are four types of screens that di§er from
8:214:16013:22:22:72:540:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:each other in the kinds of prospects and alternatives they present. To make it easier for participants
8:215:16115:22:22:72:540:643:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to correctly understand the choices we are presenting to them, we divide the experiment into four
8:216:16212:22:22:72:540:621:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:sequential parts (each with its own instruction screen), with each part containing a single type of
8:217:16311:22:22:72:540:599:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Öxed prospect and a single type of alternative. The order of the screens is randomized within each
8:218:16409:22:22:72:540:577:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:part, with half the participants completing the screens in one order, and the other half completing
8:219:16508:22:22:72:224:555:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the screens in the reverse order.
8:220:16541:22:22:88:539:533:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In Part A, the Öxed prospects are in the gain domain, and the alternatives are sure gains
8:221:16630:22:22:72:540:511:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(as in the example screens in the Introduction). There are 28 Öxed prospects that di§er both in
8:222:16725:22:22:72:540:489:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:probabilities and money amounts, which range from $0 to $400. The seven alternatives for each
8:223:16818:22:22:72:540:467:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Öxed prospect range from the Öxed prospectís certainty equivalent for a CRRA expected-utility-
8:224:16912:22:21:72:540:445:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:maximizer with CRRA parameter!=0:99to the certainty equivalent for!="1(which is risk
8:225:16996:21:22:72:540:424:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:seeking).4Because the range of estimates of!in the literature falls well within this interval (Booij,
8:226:17097:22:22:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:van Praag, and van de Kuilen, 2010), the interval likely covers the relevant range of alternatives for
8:227:17199:22:22:72:540:380:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the participants. Each participant is told that there is a 1/6 chance that one of his or her choices
8:228:17299:22:22:72:540:358:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:in Part A will be randomly selected and implemented for real stakes at the end of the experiment.
8:229:17396:22:22:72:540:336:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The expected payout for a risk-neutral participant who rolls a 6 is about $100.  The remaining
8:230:17490:22:22:72:323:314:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:parts of the experiments involve hypothetical stakes.
8:231:17543:22:22:88:539:292:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In Part B, the Öxed prospects now have outcomes in the loss domain, and the alternatives are
8:232:17635:22:22:72:540:270:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:sure losses. The 28 prospects and alternatives in Part B are identical to those in Part A but with
8:233:17733:22:22:72:241:248:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:all dollar amounts multiplied by -1.
8:234:17769:22:22:88:539:226:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Parts C and D depart somewhat from the baseline format of our experiment, in that the
8:235:17854:22:22:72:540:204:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:alternatives are now risky prospects rather than sure things. Moreover, in Part C, the Öxed prospect
8:236:17954:22:22:72:540:182:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:is the degenerate prospect of a sure thing of $0 and is not listed at the top of each screen. The
8:237:18051:22:19:72:540:160:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:seven alternatives on each of the four screens in Part C are mixed prospects that have a 50% chance
8:238:18150:19:11:72:540:141:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:implement a two-step procedure: after Önding the point at which participants switch from preferring the alternative
8:239:18265:11:11:72:540:130:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:outcomes to preferring the Öxed prospect, the participant make choices between the Öxed prospect and a second set
8:240:18378:11:11:72:540:119:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:of seven alternative outcomes, linearly spaced between a value 25% higher than the lowest amount accepted in the
8:241:18490:11:11:72:540:108:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:Örst set and a value 25% lower than the highest amount rejected. We avoid this two-step procedure (which Harrison,
8:242:18604:11:7:72:479:97:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:Lau and Rutstrˆm, 2007, call an ìIterative Multiple Price Listî) to maintain incentive compatibility.
8:243:18705:7:15:84:540:90:36:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:?:4We use#=0:99because#=1corresponds to log utility and implies a certainty equivalent of $0 for any prospect
8:244:18812:15:33:72:198:75:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:with a chance of a $0 outcome.
8:245:18842:33:0:303:308:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:8
9:246:18843:0:169:72:457:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:of a loss and 50% chance of a gain. For example, one of the screens in Part C is:
9:247:18924:169:22:72:540:540:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:On any given screen, the amount of the possible loss is Öxed, and the seven mixed prospects involve
9:248:19023:22:22:72:540:518:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:di§erent amounts of the possible gain. Part C has four screens, each with a di§erent loss amount:
9:249:19120:22:22:72:193:496:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:$25, $50, $100, and $150.
9:250:19145:22:22:88:539:474:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Part D also comprises four screens, each containing choices between a Öxed 50%-50% risky
9:251:19233:22:22:72:540:452:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:prospect and seven alternative 50%-50% risky prospects. On two of the four screens, both the Öxed
9:252:19330:22:22:72:540:430:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:prospect and the alternatives are mixed prospects, i.e., one possible outcome is a gain and the other
9:253:19431:22:183:72:206:408:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:is a loss, as in the following:
9:254:19462:183:22:72:540:225:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:On the other two screens, the Öxed and the alternative prospects involve only gains. On any given
9:255:19559:22:22:72:540:203:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:screen, one of the two possible realizations of the alternative prospect is Öxed, and the seven choices
9:256:19662:22:22:72:540:181:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:on the screen involve di§erent amounts of the other possible realization of that prospect. For each
9:257:19761:22:22:72:540:159:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:screen in Parts C and D, the alternative prospects range from the amount that would make an
9:258:19852:22:22:72:540:137:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:individual with linear utility, no probability distortion, and loss insensitivity ("=0) indi§erent to
9:259:19953:22:22:72:540:115:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the Öxed prospect to the amount that would make an individual with loss aversion"=3indi§erent.
9:260:20047:22:51:88:539:93:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:After Parts A-D, participants complete a brief questionnaire that asks age, race, educational
9:261:20140:51:0:303:308:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:9
10:262:20141:0:22:72:540:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:background, standardized test scores, ZIP code of permanent residence, and parentsí income (if
10:263:20235:22:22:72:540:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the participant is a student) or own income (if not a student).  It also asks a few self-reported
10:264:20332:22:38:72:507:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:behavioral questions, including general willingness to take risks and frequency of gambling.
10:265:20424:38:29:72:169:627:143:g_d0_f2:143:g_d0_f2:?:2.2  Treatments
10:266:20439:29:22:88:539:598:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:As detailed below, the experiment has a5#2design, with Öve ìPullî treatments, which vary
10:267:20527:22:22:72:540:576:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the set of alternatives, crossed with two ìEVî treatments, which vary whether the expected value of
10:268:20626:22:22:72:540:554:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the prospects is displayed or not. Each participant is randomly assigned to one of the ten treatment
10:269:20726:22:22:72:464:532:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:cells and remains in this cell for all screens and all parts (A-D) of the experiment.
10:270:20811:22:22:88:539:510:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The Pull treatments allow us to assess whether the compromise e§ect impacts measured risk
10:271:20900:22:22:72:540:488:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:and loss preferences. The Öve treatments are identical in the set of Öxed prospects and in the Örst
10:272:20999:22:22:72:540:466:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:and seventh alternative on each screen but di§er from each other in the intermediate (the second
10:273:21095:22:21:72:540:444:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:through sixth) alternatives.  For instance, in Part A for the illustrative Öxed prospect above in
10:274:21192:21:22:72:540:423:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the screenshots in the Introductionóa 10% chance of gaining $100 and a 90% chance of gaining
10:275:21284:22:22:72:540:401:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:$50óthe alternatives (a) through (g) are shown in the positive half of Figure 1 for all Öve Pull
10:276:21380:22:22:72:126:379:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:treatments.
10:277:21391:22:22:88:539:357:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The Öve treatments are labeled Pull -2, Pull -1, Pull 0, Pull 1, and Pull 2.  In the Pull 0
10:278:21482:22:22:72:540:335:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:treatment, the alternatives are evenly spaced, aside from rounding to the nearest $0.10, from the
10:279:21579:22:22:72:540:313:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:low amount of $53.60 to the high amount of $57.00. In the Pull 1 and the Pull 2 treatments, the
10:280:21674:22:22:72:540:291:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:intermediate alternatives are more densely concentrated at the monetary amounts closer to zero.
10:281:21769:22:22:72:540:269:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:These treatments are designed to resemble T&Kís experiment, in which the second through sixth
10:282:21862:22:22:72:540:247:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:alternatives are ìlogarithmically spaced between the extreme outcomes of the prospectî (T&K,
10:283:21954:22:22:72:540:225:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:p. 305). Conversely, in the Pull -1 and Pull -2 treatments, the intermediate alternatives are more
10:284:22052:22:22:72:540:203:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:densely concentrated at the monetary amounts farther from zero. Pull 2 and Pull -2 are more skewed
10:285:22150:22:22:72:540:181:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:than Pull 1 and Pull -1. We refer to the di§erent treatments as ìPullsî to convey the intuition
10:286:22245:22:21:72:540:159:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:that they pull the distributions of the intermediate alternatives toward zero (for the positive Pulls)
10:287:22347:21:96:72:275:138:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:or away from zero (for the negative Pulls).
10:288:22390:96:0:300:311:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:A:10
11:289:22851:0:0:300:311:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:A:11
12:290:22853:0:22:88:539:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Analogously, in Parts C and D, Pull 1 and Pull 2 pull the distribution of the varying amounts
12:291:22946:22:22:72:540:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:of the intermediate alternative prospect on each screen toward zero, and Pull -1 and Pull -2 do the
12:292:23045:22:22:72:540:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:opposite.  The Online Appendix describes the precise algorithm we use to determine the second
12:293:23138:22:22:72:540:643:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:through sixth alternatives and shows the complete set of Öxed prospects and alternatives for each
12:294:23235:22:22:72:319:621:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Pull treatment and for each part of the experiment.
12:295:23286:22:22:88:539:599:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The EV treatments di§er in whether or not we inform participants about the expected values of
12:296:23379:22:22:72:540:577:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the prospects. Because we anticipated that many participants would be unfamiliar with the concept
12:297:23476:22:22:72:540:555:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:of expected value, simple language is used in the ìEV treatmentî to describe it. For instance, in
12:298:23573:22:22:72:540:533:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Part A, the following appears below the Öxed prospect at the top of the screen: ìOn average, you
12:299:23669:22:38:72:270:511:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:would gain $55 from taking this gamble.î
12:300:23709:38:29:72:241:473:143:g_d0_f2:143:g_d0_f2:?:2.3  Procedures and Sample
12:301:23735:29:21:88:539:444:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The experiment was run online from March 11 to March 20, 2010. Our sample was drawn from
12:302:23823:21:22:72:540:423:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the Harvard Business School Computer Lab for Experimental Researchís (CLER) online subject
12:303:23913:22:22:72:540:401:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:pool database. This database contains several thousand participants nationwide who are available
12:304:24009:22:22:72:540:379:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to participate in online studies.  Participants had to be at least 18 years old, eligible to receive
12:305:24109:22:22:72:540:357:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:payment in the U.S., and not on Harvard Universityís regular payroll.  At the time we ran the
12:306:24202:22:22:72:540:335:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:experiment, members of the CLER online subject pool database were mainly recruited through
12:307:24292:22:22:72:283:313:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:áyer postings around neighboring campuses.
12:308:24334:22:22:88:539:291:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:At the launch of the experiment, the CLER lab posted a description to advertise the experiment
12:309:24428:22:22:72:540:269:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to the members of the online subject pool database. Any member of the pool could then participate
12:310:24525:22:22:72:540:247:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:until a sample size of 550 was reached.  Each participant was pseudo-randomly assigned to one
12:311:24618:22:22:72:540:225:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Pull and to one EV treatment to ensure that our treatments were well-balanced. A total of 521
12:312:24711:22:22:72:540:203:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:participants completed all four parts of the experiment. The mean response time for the participants
12:313:24811:22:22:72:406:181:119:g_d0_f3:64:g_d0_f3:C:who completed the experiment in less than one hour was 32 minutes.5
12:314:24878:22:21:88:539:159:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In addition to the above-described incentive payment for Part A, participants were paid a total
12:315:24973:21:22:72:540:138:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:of $5 if they began the experiment; $7 if they completed Part A; $9 if they completed Parts A
12:316:25066:22:16:72:540:116:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:and B; $11 if they completed Parts A, B, and C; and $15 if they completed all four parts of the
12:317:25161:16:15:84:539:100:36:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:?:5Participants were allowed to complete the experiment in more than one session and some response times exceeded
12:318:25272:15:43:72:478:85:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:24 hours. Of the 497 participants for whom we have response time data, 405 took less than an hour.
12:319:25370:43:0:300:311:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:A:12
13:320:25372:0:38:72:128:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:experiment.

14:379:27751:22:22:72:539:403:119:g_d0_f5:119:g_d0_f3:C:"qf""qAlt'"q(N(0;1). We refer tocias theparameter for the compromise e§ect of rowi, and

14:381:27933:22:22:72:540:359:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the Öxed prospect. In other words, the constraint implies that this set of parameters does not have
14:382:28032:22:22:72:540:337:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:an average e§ect (summing across all rows in the MPL) on the preference between the alternative
14:383:28127:22:22:72:182:315:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:and the Öxed prospect.
14:384:28149:22:22:88:539:293:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Our estimation strategy jointly estimates three sets of parameters:  (i) the prospect theory

14:388:28521:22:22:72:540:206:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:perspective, the scaling parameters are nuisance parameters.  The incorporation of the (varying)
14:389:28617:22:22:72:540:184:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:scaling parameters partially addresses the critique of random utility models identiÖed by Wilcox
14:390:28713:22:22:72:540:162:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(2011) and Apesteguia and Ballester (2018). Our use of varying scaling parameters follows the
14:391:28806:22:22:72:540:140:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:spirit of the recommendations of Wilcox (2011). The solution of Apesteguia and Ballester (2018) ñ
14:392:28903:22:22:72:540:118:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:stochastic preferences parameters ñ could also be incorporated into our framework, though it would
14:393:29001:22:16:72:486:96:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:involve substantial computational hurdles because we have four preference parameters.

15:397:29193:29:22:88:539:680:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:We estimate the model via Maximum Likelihood Estimation, pooling participants together
15:398:29279:22:22:72:540:658:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:and clustering the standard errors at the participant level. We impose the parameter restriction
15:399:29375:22:22:72:540:636:119:g_d0_f5:119:g_d0_f3:C:!<1. 15 of the 28 Öxed prospects in Part A have a chance of yielding $0 (and likewise for Part
15:400:29469:22:22:72:540:614:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:B). Accordingly,!+1would imply that any strictly positive alternative sure outcome would be
15:401:29560:22:22:72:540:592:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:preferred with probability 1.  Every participant in the experiment made choices ruling out such
15:402:29655:22:22:72:310:570:119:g_d0_f3:64:g_d0_f3:C:extreme risk aversion, except for one participant.7
15:403:29706:22:22:88:539:548:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:We simplify the estimation in two ways.  First, we reduce the number of0qparameters by

15:405:29885:22:22:72:540:504:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:assume thatcitakes the quadratic functional formci=50+51$i+52$i2. With this functional form,
15:406:29977:22:22:72:543:482:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the constraint47i=1ci=0implies a linear restriction among the parameters,50="451"2052,so
15:407:30065:22:22:72:278:460:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:we estimate the two parameters51and52.

15:409:30193:21:22:72:539:417:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the other parameters) with data from all screens from Parts A-D.9To do so, we assume that!,
15:410:30284:22:22:72:539:395:119:g_d0_f5:119:g_d0_f3:C:-,,are the same in the gain and loss domains. Note that!is then the coe¢cient of relative risk

15:415:30749:22:22:72:114:285:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:domain).

17:492:34178:38:29:72:418:645:143:g_d0_f2:143:g_d0_f2:?:3.5  IdentiÖcation With and Without the Pull Treatments
17:493:34233:29:22:88:539:616:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Our Öve Pull treatments are designed to identify the e§ect of the compromise e§ect on measured
17:494:34327:22:22:72:540:594:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:risk preferences. However, even without the Pull treatments, generic risk aversion experiments will
17:495:34426:22:22:72:540:572:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:be able to identify the compromise e§ect parameters. To gain intuition for this fact, consider a MPL
17:496:34526:22:22:72:540:550:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:experiment in which each screen features a di§erent level of risk aversion that elicits indi§erence at
17:497:34628:22:21:72:540:528:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the middle row of the MPL. Accordingly,measuredrisk aversion will vary across screens (unless the
17:498:34725:21:22:72:540:507:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:researcher takes account of the compromise e§ect). Hence, the compromise e§ect will be identiÖed
17:499:34821:22:22:72:540:485:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:as long as (i) the compromise-e§ect parameters are included in the model, and (ii) the level of
17:500:34916:22:22:72:540:463:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:risk aversion that elicits indi§erence in the middle row varies across MPL screens.  Because the
17:501:35012:22:22:72:540:441:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:compromise e§ect parameters would be identiÖed even without within-subject variation in the Pull
17:502:35108:22:22:72:540:419:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:treatment, our data could also be used to identify the compromise e§ects at the level of each
17:503:35201:22:22:72:540:397:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:individual participant, but those estimates would be less precise than the representative agent
17:504:35296:22:45:72:274:375:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:estimates on which we focus in this paper.
17:505:35338:45:33:72:194:330:206:g_d0_f2:206:g_d0_f2:?:4Hypotheses
17:506:35349:33:22:88:539:297:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Having deÖned the model, we now articulate a number of hypotheses that we will test empirically
17:507:35444:22:22:72:540:275:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:by estimating the model with the data from the experiment.  Drawing on prior work (see the
17:508:35534:22:22:72:540:253:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Introduction for discussion), our starting point is the hypothesis that participants will be biased
17:509:35633:22:34:72:461:231:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:toward switching close to the middle of the seven rows in the Multiple Price List.
17:510:35715:34:22:72:539:197:119:g_d0_f4:119:g_d0_f3:?:Hypothesis 1:Estimates ofciwill reveal a compromise e§ect.  SpeciÖcally,^ciwill be positive
17:511:35806:22:22:72:540:175:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:in the top rows, close to zero in the middle rows, and negative in the bottom rows, decreasing
17:512:35900:22:34:72:283:153:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:monotonically from the Örst to the last row.
17:513:35944:34:21:88:539:119:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Note that a positive value ofciimplies a bias in favor of choosing the alternative (which is in the
17:514:36043:21:56:72:540:98:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:right-hand-side column of the MPL), and a negative value ofciimplies a bias in favor of choosing
17:515:36139:56:0:300:311:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:A:17
18:516:36141:0:22:72:540:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the Öxed prospects (which is in the left-hand-side column of the MPL). So Hypothesis 1 implies a
18:517:36237:22:22:72:372:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:switch point that is biased toward the middle row of the MPL.
18:518:36298:22:22:88:539:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Thus, the compromise e§ect implies that measured risk aversion in the gain domain, as assessed
18:519:36392:22:22:72:540:643:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:in Part A, will be systematically increased across the range of treatments from Pull -2 to Pull 2
18:520:36489:22:22:72:540:621:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(in the model without the compromise e§ect). For instance, consider the two example screenshots
18:521:36584:22:22:72:540:599:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:from the Introduction. The Örst screenshot illustrates the Pull -2 treatment. Since the intermediate
18:522:36684:22:22:72:540:577:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:alternatives are shifted away from zero, the compromise e§ect induces participants to choose an
18:523:36779:22:22:72:540:555:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:indi§erence point that is farther from zero, thereby implying a relatively low level of risk aversion. In
18:524:36884:22:22:72:540:533:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:contrast, in the Pull 2 treatment, illustrated in the second screenshot, the intermediate alternatives
18:525:36986:22:22:72:540:511:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:are shifted closer to zero. The compromise e§ect causes participants to choose an indi§erence point
18:526:37085:22:22:72:442:489:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:that is closer to zero, thereby implying a relatively high level of risk aversion.
18:527:37167:22:22:88:539:467:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The hypothesized e§ect of the Pull treatments on measured riskseekingin the loss domain is
18:528:37257:22:21:72:540:445:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:analogous. Moving across the range of treatments from Pull -2 to Pull 2 is now hypothesized to
18:529:37351:21:22:72:540:424:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:raise estimated risk seeking. For example, consider a Öxed prospect that has outcomes in the loss
18:530:37448:22:22:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:domain. In the Pull -2 treatment, the intermediate alternatives are all negative and shifted away
18:531:37545:22:22:72:540:380:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:from zero, coaxing participants to choose an indi§erence point that is farther from zero, thereby
18:532:37642:22:22:72:540:358:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:implying a relatively low level of risk seeking. By contrast, in the Pull 2 treatment, the intermediate
18:533:37745:22:22:72:540:336:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:alternatives are all negative and shifted relatively close to zero, coaxing participants to choose an
18:534:37846:22:22:72:524:314:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:indi§erence point that is closer to zero, thereby implying a relatively high level of risk seeking.
18:535:37945:22:22:88:539:292:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Similar considerations imply that moving across the range of treatments from Pull -2 to Pull 2
18:536:38039:22:22:72:347:270:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:is predicted to reduce the level of estimated loss aversion.
18:537:38099:22:22:88:539:248:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:We thus hypothesize that the compromise e§ect a§ects estimates of risk aversion and loss aver-
18:538:38193:22:22:72:540:226:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:sion in the traditional CPT model. In Section 3.2 above, we introduced a model that incorporates
18:539:38289:22:22:72:540:204:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:parameters for the compromise e§ect. If that model is properly speciÖed, we would expect the bias
18:540:38386:22:22:72:540:182:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:induced by the compromise e§ect to disappear and the estimates of risk aversion and loss aversion
18:541:38483:22:33:72:389:160:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to be similar across Pull treatments. In summary, we hypothesize:
18:542:38548:33:22:72:539:127:119:g_d0_f4:119:g_d0_f3:?:Hypothesis 2.a:Estimates of relative risk aversion in the gain domain (!,!+) and relative risk

29:747:53553:0:29:72:556:709:206:g_d0_f2:206:g_d0_f2:?:8DemographicCorrelatesoftheCPTModelParametersandof
29:748:53603:29:33:96:470:680:206:g_d0_f2:206:g_d0_f2:?:the Parameters that Capture the Compromise E§ect
29:749:53651:33:22:88:539:647:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:A large literature seeks to estimate the demographic correlates of economic preferences and
29:750:53742:22:22:72:540:625:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:decision making (e.g., Beauchamp, Cesarini, and Johannesson 2017, Benjamin, Brown, and Shapiro
29:751:53836:22:22:72:540:603:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:2013, Booij, van Praag, and van de Kuilen 2010, Dohmen, Falk, Hu§man, and Sunde 2010). The
29:752:53926:22:22:72:540:581:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:data we collected in our experiment, which include a number of demographic variables, allow us to
29:753:54023:22:22:72:540:559:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:contribute to this literature by analyzing the demographic correlates of the four key parameters of
29:754:54122:22:22:72:539:537:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the CPT model (!,",-,,) and of the two model parameters that capture the compromise e§ect (51,
29:755:54216:22:22:72:540:515:119:g_d0_f5:119:g_d0_f3:C:52). In our baseline demographic speciÖcation, we estimate our CPT model with the compromise
29:756:54308:22:22:72:540:493:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:e§ect using data from Parts A-D together, with these six key model parameters speciÖed as linear
29:757:54404:22:21:72:540:471:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:functions of a constant, age, sex, a dummy variable indicating whether one has a college degree,
29:758:54500:21:22:72:540:450:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:SAT Math score, the log of oneís parentsí combined annual income, as well as dummy variables to
29:759:54595:22:22:72:540:428:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:control for race. We also estimated several additional speciÖcations to verify the robustness of the
29:760:54695:22:22:72:540:406:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:results from our baseline demographic speciÖcation. First, we estimated the baseline demographic
29:761:54791:22:22:72:540:384:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:speciÖcation again, but using data from Part A only, and then using data from Part B only. Second,
29:762:54889:22:22:72:540:362:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:we estimated a speciÖcation akin to the baseline demographic speciÖcation using data from Parts A-
29:763:54987:22:22:72:540:340:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:D together, but with CARA (a.k.a. ìexponentialî) utility (Kˆbberling and Wakker 2005). Lastly,
29:764:55081:22:22:72:540:318:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:we employed a two-step procedure in which we Örst estimated our baseline CPT model with the
29:765:55172:22:22:72:540:296:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:compromise e§ect separately for each participant, and then regressed each estimated parameter of
29:766:55268:22:22:72:540:274:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:interest on the demographic variables. To reduce the number of parameters and thereby improve
29:767:55361:22:22:72:539:252:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the frequency of convergence in the Örst step of that procedure, we assume that0qis identical
29:768:55454:22:22:72:327:230:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:across all screens (for each experimental participant).
29:769:55509:22:22:88:539:208:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Two main results stand out across the baseline and robustness speciÖcations.  First, higher
29:770:55600:22:21:72:540:186:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:SAT Math scores are associated with lower!ói.e., with lower risk aversion in the gain domain and
29:771:55696:21:22:72:540:165:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:higher risk aversion (or, equivalently, lower risk seeking) in the loss domain. This result is consistent
29:772:55801:22:22:72:540:143:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:with the existing literature on the association between cognitive ability and risk preferences (see
29:773:55900:22:22:72:540:121:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Dohmen, Falk, Hu§man, and Sunde 2018 for a review of the literature), although it has been argued
29:774:55997:22:22:72:540:99:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:that this association is driven by the fact that measurement noise may be higher for individuals with
29:775:56098:22:35:72:540:77:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:lower cognitive ability (Andersson, Holm, Tyran, and Wengstrˆm 2016). The second result that
29:776:56190:35:0:300:311:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:A:29
30:777:56192:0:22:72:539:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:stands out is that higher SAT Math scores are associated withhigherloss aversion ("). This result,
30:778:56290:22:22:72:540:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:although robust across our speciÖcations, is surprising given that previous research has found that
30:779:56389:22:22:72:540:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:education is negatively associated with loss aversion (Booij, van Praag, and van de Kuilen 2010,
30:780:56485:22:22:72:540:643:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:G‰chter, Johnson, Herrmann 2007, Hjorth and Fosgerau 2011). Aside from these two results, the
30:781:56578:22:22:72:540:621:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:associations between the other covariates and parameters were not statistically distinguishable from
30:782:56678:22:22:72:285:599:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:zero or were not robust across speciÖcations.
30:783:56723:22:22:88:539:577:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:The Online Appendix reports estimates of the baseline demographic speciÖcation and provides
30:784:56814:22:22:72:540:555:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:additional details. We note that one limitation of this analysis is that our sample of experimental
30:785:56913:22:45:72:397:533:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:participants was not selected to be representative of the population.
30:786:56982:45:29:72:556:488:206:g_d0_f2:206:g_d0_f2:?:9E§ectofDisplayingtheGamblesíExpectedValuesonEsti-
30:787:57032:29:33:96:263:459:206:g_d0_f2:206:g_d0_f2:?:mated Risk Preferences
30:788:57054:33:22:88:539:426:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Displaying the expected value may anchor the participants on the expected value (Tversky and
30:789:57146:22:22:72:540:404:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Kahneman, 1974) or simplify comprehension of the gamble (Benjamin, Brown, and Shapiro, 2013),
30:790:57239:22:22:72:540:382:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:thereby making observed preferences more risk neutral. We therefore hypothesize that (1)^!+and
30:791:57333:22:22:72:523:360:119:g_d0_f10:119:g_d0_f3:?:^!!will shift toward 0 in the EV treatment, and (2)^"will shift toward 1 in the EV treatment.
30:792:57426:22:22:88:540:338:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Online Appendix Figures 1-4 show estimates of^!and^"for the subsamples corresponding to the
30:793:57517:22:22:72:540:316:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:two EV treatments, with 95% conÖdence intervals. Displaying the expected value does not appear
30:794:57611:22:19:72:540:294:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:to a§ect estimated risk preferences or loss aversion. In addition, none of the estimates of^>$2and of
30:795:57712:19:24:72:539:275:119:g_d0_f10:119:g_d0_f3:?:^>42in Table 4 are statistically distinguishable from zero. Thus, like Lichtenstein, Slovic, and Zink
30:796:57813:24:22:72:540:251:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(1969) and Montgomery and Adelbratt (1982) but unlike Harrison and Rutstrˆm (2008), we do not
30:797:57906:22:22:72:540:229:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Önd support for the hypothesis that the EV treatment shifts^!+and^!!toward 0 and^"toward
30:798:57994:22:22:72:540:207:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:1. A di§erence between our experiment and Harrison and Rutstrˆmís (2008) is that the prospects
30:799:58088:22:22:72:540:185:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:in the latter are more complex, involving four possible outcomes. It is possible that participants
30:800:58186:22:22:72:540:163:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:intuitively estimate the prospectsí expected values in our experiment but are not able to accurately
30:801:58286:22:22:72:540:141:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:do so in Harrison and Rutstrˆmís experiment, and that providing expected value information is
30:802:58379:22:77:72:341:119:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:therefore redundant in our experiment but not in theirs.
30:803:58435:77:0:300:311:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:A:30
31:804:58437:0:33:72:181:709:206:g_d0_f2:206:g_d0_f2:?:10  Conclusion
31:805:58451:33:22:88:539:676:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:In this paper, we estimate an econometric model that explicitly takes into account the compro-
31:806:58545:22:22:72:540:654:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:mise e§ect and thus disentangles it from risk preference parameters. The resulting risk-preference
31:807:58643:22:22:72:540:632:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:estimates are robust: the inferred risk parameters essentially do not change with exogenous ma-
31:808:58738:22:22:72:540:610:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:nipulations of the compromise e§ect.  Without parameters for the compromise e§ect, however,
31:809:58829:22:22:72:540:588:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:we replicate the Önding from prior work that risk-preference estimates are sensitive to exogenous
31:810:58926:22:22:72:262:566:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:manipulations of the compromise e§ect.
31:811:58964:22:22:88:539:544:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:How do our ìdebiasedî preference-parameter estimates (from Table 1) compare to those from the
31:812:59057:22:22:72:539:522:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:literature? For risk aversion in the gain and loss domains, we estimate^!+=0:448and^!!="0:106,
31:813:59151:22:21:72:540:500:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:respectively. Booij, van Praag, and van de Kuilenís (2010) Table 1 reviews existing experimental
31:814:59247:21:22:72:540:479:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:estimates. Translated into the CRRA functional form we estimate, the range of existing parameter
31:815:59343:22:22:72:540:457:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:estimates is^!+2["0:01;0:78]in the gain domain and^!!2["0:06;0:39]in the loss domain. For
31:816:59432:22:22:72:540:435:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:loss aversion, we estimateb"=1:311. Although T&K estimated"to be2:25, the literature contains
31:817:59525:22:22:72:540:413:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:a broad range of estimates: among the papers reviewed by Abdellaoui, Bleichrodt, and Paraschiv
31:818:59619:22:22:72:539:391:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(2007, Tables 1 and 5),^"2[0:74;8:27], and among those reviewed by Booij, van Praag, and van
31:819:59711:22:22:72:539:369:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:de Kuilen (2010, Table 1),^"2[1:07;2:61].  Finally, our estimates of the two-parameter Prelec
31:820:59804:22:22:72:539:347:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(1998) probability-weighting parameters are in the rangesb-2[0:564;0:690]andb,2[0:858;1:471].
31:821:59897:22:22:72:540:325:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:Booij, van Praag, and van de Kuilenís (2010) Table 1 only lists three studies that estimated this
31:822:59994:22:22:72:540:303:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:functional form, and they only did so for prospects in the gain domain. The ranges of estimates
31:823:60089:22:22:72:540:281:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:are^-+2[0:53;1:05]and^,+2[1:08;2:12]. Fox and Poldrackís (2014) Table A.3 also lists three
31:824:60179:22:22:72:540:259:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:studies that estimated the two-parameter Prelec (1998) functional form for prospects in the gain
31:825:60275:22:22:72:539:237:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:domain.  The ranges of estimates are^-+2[0:62;1:15]and^,+2[1:00;1:58].  Overall, then, our
31:826:60365:22:21:72:540:215:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:parameter estimates are broadly in line with existing estimates in the literature, except that some
31:827:60464:21:22:72:539:194:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:of our estimates of the probability weighting parameter,+and our estimate of risk aversion in
31:828:60557:22:22:72:539:172:119:g_d0_f3:64:g_d0_f17:?:the loss domain!!fall below the range of estimates in the literatureóindeed, our estimate of!!
31:829:60651:22:22:72:436:150:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:indicates riskaversionin the loss domain, the opposite of CPTís prediction.
31:830:60726:22:22:88:539:128:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:As in T&K, our estimation of the prospect-theory parameters has assumed that the reference
31:831:60816:22:22:72:540:106:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:point is the participantís status-quo wealth.  Kˆszegi and Rabin (2006, 2007) have argued that
31:832:60910:22:42:72:540:84:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the assumption that the reference point is the participantís (possibly stochastic) expectation of
31:833:61007:42:0:300:311:42:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:A:31
32:834:61009:0:22:72:540:709:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:wealth provides a better explanation of risk-taking behavior in a variety of contexts.  Could a
32:835:61104:22:22:72:540:687:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:version of prospect theory in which the reference point reáects a participantís expectations explain
32:836:61204:22:22:72:540:665:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:why the manipulations of the choice set ináuence the estimated preference parameters (when we
32:837:61297:22:22:72:540:643:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:do not include parameters for the compromise e§ect)? This question poses a challenging research
32:838:61392:22:22:72:540:621:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:program. Modeling the reference point as an expectation would not merely make the reference point
32:839:61489:22:22:72:540:599:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:depend on the alternative options in the current choice problem but also on the sequence of choice
32:840:61587:22:22:72:540:577:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:problems that have been faced already, as well as the experimental instructions.  Existing work
32:841:61682:22:22:72:540:555:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:provides little guidance on modeling these complex relationships, and many ad hoc assumptions
32:842:61775:22:22:72:162:533:119:g_d0_f3:64:g_d0_f3:C:would be needed.13
32:843:61793:22:22:88:539:511:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:A limitation of our paper is that the compromise-e§ect parameter values we estimate are spe-
32:844:61885:22:22:72:540:489:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:ciÖc to our experimental setting, and thus cannot be extrapolated to other settings (see related
32:845:61981:22:22:72:540:467:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:points in Levitt and List 2007). For example, our experiment includes 64 MPLís, which may in-
32:846:62074:22:21:72:540:445:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:duce fatigue among experimental participants, potentially explaining why the compromise e§ect
32:847:62167:21:22:72:540:424:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:strengthens from Part A to Part B. However, the methodology we demonstrateójointly estimating
32:848:62260:22:22:72:540:402:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the compromise e§ect and preference parametersóis general and can be applied and extended in
32:849:62352:22:22:72:219:380:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:at least three useful directions.
32:850:62385:22:22:88:539:358:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:First, the compromise-e§ect controls that we propose here can be used not only to improve
32:851:62474:22:22:72:540:336:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:the robustness of estimates of risk preference parameters, but also of parameter estimates for any
32:852:62572:22:22:72:540:314:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:other preferences elicited using MPLs, including time and other-regarding preferences.  Second,
32:853:62667:22:22:72:540:292:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:our method can be applied to other settings where the compromise e§ect may play a role, such
32:854:62759:22:22:72:540:270:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:as Choi, Fisman, Gale, and Karivís (2007) graphical interface for eliciting preferences, Andreoni
32:855:62856:22:22:72:540:248:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:and Sprengerís (2012) convex time budget procedure for eliciting time preferences, or consumer
32:856:62950:22:22:72:540:226:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:choices in the kinds of settings that originally motivated the psychology and marketing research
32:857:63046:22:22:72:540:204:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:on the compromise e§ect (Simonson 1989). Finally, the same econometric procedure we implement
32:858:63139:22:22:72:540:182:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:hereóestimating a discrete-choice model that includes additional parameters that capture location
32:859:63236:22:21:72:540:160:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:in the choice setócould also be applied to measure and control for other types of context e§ects,
32:860:63333:21:22:72:540:139:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:such as a tendency to choose items that happen to come at the beginning of a list of alternatives
32:861:63430:22:17:72:348:117:119:g_d0_f3:119:g_d0_f3:C:(e.g., as in election ballots; e.g., Koppell and Steen 2004).
32:862:63491:17:15:80:539:100:36:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:?:13Sprenger (2015) assumes that the Öxed prospect in each binary choice pins down a participantís reference point.
32:863:63604:15:11:72:540:85:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:Because the Öxed prospect was held constant across our scale manipulations, this approach canít explain the e§ects
32:864:63718:11:32:72:103:74:80:g_d0_f3:80:g_d0_f3:C:we Önd.

50:1275:88184:14:14:90:525:482:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:treatments  involve  different  sets  of  choices,  and  the  raw  data  are  thus  not  directly 
50:1276:88282:14:13:90:525:468:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:comparable across treatments. For example, consider Online Appendix Figure 3.1, which 
50:1277:88368:13:14:90:525:455:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:shows the percentages of safe choices in Part A. The percentages are lower in the Pull 2 
50:1278:88457:14:14:90:525:441:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:treatment and higher in the Pull -2 treatment. Recall that the alternative prospects in the 
50:1279:88549:14:14:90:525:427:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:Pull 2 treatment involve amounts that are closer to zero, and the alternative prospects in 
50:1280:88640:14:14:90:526:413:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:the Pull -2 treatment involve amounts that are further away from zero. In the absence of a 
50:1281:88731:14:14:90:525:399:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:compromise  effect,  a  participant  with  a  given  certainty  equivalent  for  a  gamble  on  a 
50:1282:88829:14:13:90:525:385:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:given screen will thus select the safe option less frequently in the Pull 2 treatment than in 
50:1283:88923:13:14:90:526:372:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:the Pull -2 treatment. The existence of a compromise effect would partially mitigate this 
50:1284:89013:14:14:90:526:358:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:tendency  but  would  not  fully  counter  it. Because of  this,  Online  Appendix  Figure  3.1 
50:1285:89108:14:14:90:526:344:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:shows  that  the  percentage  of  safe  choices  decreases  in Pull,  even  though  theoretical 
50:1286:89203:14:14:90:525:330:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:considerations suggest (see Section 4 of the main text), and our empirical results confirm, 
50:1287:89295:14:14:90:397:316:144:g_d0_f54:144:g_d0_f54:C:that estimates of risk aversion (i.e., !, !", !#) increase in Pull.
